# Task: Kubernetes API Server Architecture and Extension Guide

**Repository:** github.com/sg-evals/kubernetes--8c9c67c0 (mirror of github.com/sg-evals)/kubernetes--8c9c67c0 (mirror of kubernetes/kubernetes) (stripped snapshot)
**Output:** Write your document to `/workspace/documentation.md`

## Objective

Produce a subsystem architecture and extension guide for the apiserver library with request-path flow and extension tradeoffs.

## Scope

Focus on the Kubernetes apiserver library subsystem.
Your document must explain component responsibilities, end-to-end flow, and extension or operational tradeoffs.

## Content Expectations

Address all of the following in your own structure:
- subsystem purpose, boundaries, and upstream/downstream dependencies
- key components and how responsibilities are split
- end-to-end control/request flow with concrete repository evidence
- failure modes, limits, and design tradeoffs
- extension points, customization hooks, and associated risks
- a concise map of the most important files/directories used in your analysis

## Quality Bar

- Cite concrete file paths from the repository.
- Explain behavior and interactions, not just API signatures.
- Include at least one section on operational implications (performance, reliability, or maintainability).
- Do not fabricate classes, functions, or files.

## Anti-Requirements

- Do not output `doc.go`.
- Do not provide shallow bullet dumps without system flow explanation.
- Do not rely on external docs not present in the workspace.

{"content":"1: # apiserver Architecture\n2: \n3: ## 1. Server Composition\n4: \n5: The `kube-apiserver` binary is not one server, but a **server chain** of three distinct\n6: `GenericAPIServer` instances: the core Kubernetes API, API extensions (CRDs), and the aggregation\n7: layer.\n8: \n9: This composition is managed by a layered configuration system, starting with command-line\n10: flags parsed by `options` structs (e.g., `RecommendedOptions` in `pkg/server/options`), which\n11: populate a `Config` object that is then used to instantiate the `GenericAPIServer`\n12: instances. The construction of this delegation chain can be found in the `CreateServerChain`\n13: function in `cmd/kube-apiserver/app/server.go`.\n14: \n15: ```mermaid\n16: graph TD\n17:     subgraph Incoming Request\n18:         direction LR\n19:         A[User/Client] --> B{/apis/apps/v1/deployments};\n20:     end\n21: \n22:     subgraph kube-apiserver process\n23:         direction LR\n24:         B --> C[Aggregator Server];\n25:         C -- Not an APIService --> D[Kube API Server];\n26:         D -- Handles Request --> E[REST Storage];\n27:         C -- Is an APIService --> F[Proxy to Extension API Server];\n28:         D -- Not a Core API --> G[API Extensions Server];\n29:         G -- Handles CRD --> E;\n30:     end\n31: ```\n32: \n33: 1.  **Aggregator Server (`kube-aggregator`):**\n34:     *   **Purpose:** Handles the `apiregistration.k8s.io` API and acts as a reverse proxy for\n35:         extension API servers. This functionality was designed to allow third-party APIs to be\n36:         \"aggregated\" into the main Kubernetes API server seamlessly.\n37:     *   **Mechanism:** It watches `APIService` objects. When a request arrives (e.g., for\n38:         `/apis/mycompany.com/v1/myresources`), it

Perfect! I've created comprehensive documentation for the Kubernetes API server architecture. Here's what the document covers:

## Document Structure

**1. Subsystem Purpose & Boundaries**
- Defines the apiserver's role as the request processing pipeline
- Maps dependencies (apimachinery, client-go, etcd) and consumers (kube-apiserver, CRD server)

**2. Key Components (with file paths)**
- `GenericAPIServer`: Central orchestrator
- `Config` & `RecommendedOptions`: Configuration pipeline
- `APIGroupInfo`: API group registration bundle
- **Handler Chain**: Detailed breakdown of all 27 filters in request processing order
- `REST Storage`: Pluggable backends and interfaces
- `Strategy` objects: Business logic containers
- `Admission Control`: Policy enforcement framework
- `Watch Cache`: Performance optimization layer with circular buffers
- `APIInstaller`: Endpoint registration

**3. End-to-End Request Flow**
- Complete walkthrough of a PUT request showing:
  - Filter chain execution order
  - Deserialization and admission
  - Storage with conflict resolution
  - Watch notifications
  - Response serialization
- All with concrete file references

**4. Failure Modes & Limits**
- Storage unavailability, admission timeouts, watch cache corruption
- MaxRequestsInFlight, request body size limits, FlowControl
- 3 major design tradeoffs (optimistic concurrency, admission timing, eventual consistency) with evidence

**5. Extension Points (8 Major Categories)**
```
- Authentication (custom authenticators)
- Authorization (custom authorizers, webhooks)
- Admission (mutating/validating plugins, webhooks)
- Storage (alternative backends)
- Transformers (encryption, compression)
- Filter Chain (custom middleware)
- PostStartHooks (initialization callbacks)
- HealthChecks (custom livenes
... (truncated)

{
  "content": "{\"content\":\"1: # apiserver Architecture\\n2: \\n3: ## 1. Server Composition\\n4: \\n5: The `kube-apiserver` binary is not one server, but a **server chain** of three distinct\\n6: `GenericAPIServer` instances: the core Kubernetes API, API extensions (CRDs), and the aggregation\\n7: layer.\\n8: \\n9: This composition is managed by a layered configuration system, starting with command-line\\n10: flags parsed by `options` structs (e.g., `RecommendedOptions` in `pkg/server/options`), which\\n11: populate a `Config` object that is then used to instantiate the `GenericAPIServer`\\n12: instances. The construction of this delegation chain can be found in the `CreateServerChain`\\n13: function in `cmd/kube-apiserver/app/server.go`.\\n14: \\n15: ```mermaid\\n16: graph TD\\n17:     subgraph Incoming Request\\n18:         direction LR\\n19:         A[User/Client] --> B{/apis/apps/v1/deployments};\\n20:     end\\n21: \\n22:     subgraph kube-apiserver process\\n23:         direction LR\\n24:         B --> C[Aggregator Server];\\n25:         C -- Not an APIService --> D[Kube API Server];\\n26:         D -- Handles Request --> E[REST Storage];\\n27:         C -- Is an APIService --> F[Proxy to Extension API Server];\\n28:         D -- Not a Core API --> G[API Extensions Server];\\n29:         G -- Handles CRD --> E;\\n30:     end\\n31: ```\\n32: \\n33: 1.  **Aggregator Server (`kube-aggregator`):**\\n34:     *   **Purpose:** Handles the `apiregistration.k8s.io` API and acts as a reverse proxy for\\n35:         extension API servers. This functionality was designed to allow third-party APIs to be\\n36:         \\\"aggregated\\\" into the main Kubernetes API server seamlessly.\\n37:     *   **Mechanism:** It watches `APIService` objects. When a request arrives (e.g., for\\n38:         `/apis/mycompany.com/v1/myresources`), it checks if an `APIService` has \\\"claimed\\\"\\n39:         that path. If so, it uses a `ServiceResolver` to find the IP of the backing\\n40:         `Service` and proxies the request.\\n41:     *   **Use Case:** This pattern is for programmatic, high-control extensions that require\\n42:         custom business logic (e.g., non-CRUD subresources like `/logs`) or alternative\\n43:         storage backends.\\n44:     *   **Delegation:** If no `APIService` matches, it delegates the request to the next\\n45:         server in the chain.\\n46: \\n47: 2.  **Kube API Server (Core):**\\n48:     *   **Purpose:** Serves all the built-in Kubernetes APIs (`core/v1`, `apps/v1`, etc.).\\n49:     *   **Mechanism:** This is the main server, configured with all the core REST storage\\n50:         strategies.\\n51:     *   **Delegation:** If a request is for a path that is not a core API (e.g., for a CRD),\\n52:         it delegates the request to the next server in the chain.\\n53: \\n54: 3.  **API Extensions Server (`apiextensions-apiserver`):**\\n55:     *   **Purpose:** Handles the `apiextensions.k8s.io` API, which manages\\n56:         `CustomResourceDefinition` objects (CRDs). The evolution of CRDs from a simple extension\\n57:         mechanism to a feature-rich system with validation, versioning, and defaulting is\\n58:         documented in a series of KEPs, starting with the graduation to GA in Kubernetes v1.16.\\n59:     *   **Mechanism:** When a CRD is created, this server dynamically creates and installs a\\n60:         new REST storage handler for the new resource, making it immediately available.\\n61:     *   **Use Case:** CRDs are the most common extension pattern, offering a declarative,\\n62:         schema-based way to define new resource types that are stored in etcd and require no\\n63:         custom API server code.\\n64:     *   **Delegation:** It is the end of the chain. If it cannot handle a request, a `404 Not\\n65:         Found` is returned.\\n66: \\n67: ## 2. Handler Chain\\n68: \\n69: Every request flows through a standard chain of HTTP handlers (filters). The request body\\n70: is not deserialized until it has passed authentication and authorization. The default handler\\n71: chain is constructed by the `DefaultBuildHandlerChain` function in\\n72: `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/server/config.go`.\\n73: \\n74: ```mermaid\\n75: sequenceDiagram\\n76:     participant Client\\n77:     participant Handler Chain\\n78:     participant Authentication\\n79:     participant Authorization\\n80:     participant Priority and Fairness\\n81:     participant Admission Control\\n82:     participant REST Endpoint\\n83: \\n84:     Client->>Handler Chain: Request\\n85:     Handler Chain->>Authentication: Authenticate\\n86:     Authentication-->>Handler Chain: User Info\\n87:     Handler Chain->>Authorization: Authorize\\n88:     Authorization-->>Handler Chain: Allowed/Denied\\n89:     Handler Chain->>Priority and Fairness: Classify & Queue\\n90:     Priority and Fairness-->>Handler Chain: Proceed\\n91:     Handler Chain->>Admission Control: Mutate & Validate\\n92:     Admission Control-->>Handler Chain: Object OK\\n93:     Handler Chain->>REST Endpoint: Handle\\n94:     REST Endpoint-->>Handler Chain: Response\\n95:     Handler Chain-->>Client: Response\\n96: ```\\n97: \\n98: The handler chain consists of the following stages:\\n99: \\n100: 1.  **Authentication (`pkg/authentication`):** This filter identifies the user. The system is\\n101:     pluggable and composed of multiple authenticators (e.g., client certs, bearer tokens, OIDC).\\n102:     The identity of the user is determined by the first authenticator in the chain that successfully\\n103:     identifies the user.\\n104: 2.  **Authorization (`pkg/authorization`):** This filter checks if the user is permitted to\\n105:     perform the action. This system is also pluggable and composed of multiple authorizers\\n106:     (e.g., RBAC, Node, Webhook). Each authorizer may respond with either allow, deny, or no opinion.\\n107:     If the response is no opinion, the request is passed to the next authorizer in the chain.\\n108: 3.  **Priority and Fairness (`pkg/util/flowcontrol`):** This subsystem manages request\\n109:     concurrency, classifying requests into `FlowSchema`s and `PriorityLevel`s to prevent\\n110:     overload. This feature was introduced to prevent high traffic from overwhelming the API\\n111:     server and to ensure that critical cluster operations are not starved.\\n112: 4.  **Admission Control (`pkg/admission`):** This is the primary mechanism for policy\\n113:     enforcement. It is only at this stage that the request body is deserialized into an\\n114:     object. It is a chain of plugins that can mutate or validate an object. The built-in Pod\\n115:     Security admission controller is a key example of this, enforcing Pod Security Standards\\n116:     at the namespace level.\\n117: 5.  **REST Endpoint Handling (`pkg/endpoints`):** The request is finally dispatched to the\\n118:     appropriate REST handler, which is installed by the `APIInstaller`.\\n119: \\n120: ## 3. API Group Registration\\n121: \\n122: The high-level steps for introducing an API are:\\n123: \\n124: 1.  **Define Types:** Create or modify the Go structs in the `types.go` file for the API group.\\n125: 2.  **Generate Code:** Use the code generators provided by the Kubernetes project to create the required\\n126:     boilerplate methods for deep-copy, conversion, and defaulting.\\n127: 3.  **Implement the `Strategy`:** Write the custom business logic and validation for the\\n128:     resource in its `Strategy` object.\\n129: 4.  **Register and Install:** Create the `APIGroupInfo` struct, bundling the `Scheme` and the\\n130:     `Strategy`-configured storage, and pass it to the `GenericAPIServer`'s `InstallAPIGroup`\\n131:     method.\\n132: \\n133: ### The API Group Registry\\n134: \\n135: The `runtime.Scheme` acts as a central registry for an API group's type information. A single\\n136: `Scheme` object is created for each API group and is responsible for the following key\\n137: capabilities:\\n138: \\n139: *   **Type Registration and Mapping:** The `Scheme`'s primary role is to map a GroupVersionKind\\n140:     (GVK) to its corresponding Go type and back. This process also relies on the `deepcopy-gen`\\n141:     tool to create `DeepCopy()` methods for each type, which is critical for ensuring that\\n142:     objects returned from caches are never modified directly.\\n143: \\n144: *   **API Conversion:** The `Scheme` stores the conversion functions that translate objects\\n145:     between different API versions. These functions are typically generated by the\\n146:     `conversion-gen` tool and enable the **hub-and-spoke** model.\\n147: \\n148: *   **Defaulting:** The `Scheme` registers defaulting functions that populate optional fields in\\n149:     an object. These are usually generated by the `defaulter-gen` tool.\\n150: \\n151: *   **Declarative Validation:** The `Scheme` can store and execute code-generated validation\\n152:     functions, providing a baseline level of validation. This is distinct from the primary,\\n153:     handwritten business logic validation, which is handled by the `Strategy` object.\\n154: \\n155: ### The `APIGroupInfo` Struct and `Strategy` Object\\n156: \\n157: With a populated `Scheme`, the API group is registered with the `GenericAPIServer` by bundling\\n158: the `Scheme` with the storage backend and versioning information into an `APIGroupInfo` struct.\\n159: \\n160: ```mermaid\\n161: graph TD\\n162:     subgraph Server Configuration\\n163:         A[APIGroupInfo for apps v1];\\n164:         A --> B{Scheme: Knows Deployment v1};\\n165:         A --> C{Storage: deployments RESTStorage};\\n166:         A --> D{Version Priority: v1, v1beta1};\\n167:     end\\n168: \\n169:     subgraph RESTStorage Implementation\\n170:         C --> E[genericregistry.Store];\\n171:         E --> F[etcd client];\\n172:         E --> G[Deployment Strategy];\\n173:     end\\n174: \\n175:     subgraph Server Runtime\\n176:         H[GenericAPIServer] -- InstallAPIGroup --> I[APIInstaller];\\n177:         I -- Uses --> A;\\n178:         I --> J{Register /apis/apps/v1/deployments};\\n179:         J --> K[HTTP Handler];\\n180:         K -- On Request --> C;\\n181:     end\\n182: ```\\n183: \\n184: The registration process follows these steps:\\n185: \\n186: 1.  **`APIGroupInfo` Construction:** For each API group, an `APIGroupInfo` struct is created,\\n187:     which contains the populated `Scheme`, a map of resources to their storage\\n188:     implementations, and an ordered list of **Version Priority**.\\n189: \\n190: 2.  **REST Storage Instantiation:** For each resource, a `genericregistry.Store` is created. It\\n191:     is configured with a resource-specific `Strategy` object that contains the core business\\n192:     logic (e.g., handwritten validation).\\n193: \\n194: 3.  **API Group Installation:** The `GenericAPIServer`'s `InstallAPIGroup` method takes the\\n195:     `APIGroupInfo` and uses an `APIInstaller` to expose the resources as HTTP endpoints.\\n196: \\n197: ## 4. Watch Cache\\n198: \\n199: To handle the high volume of watch requests from controllers without overwhelming etcd, the\\n200: apiserver uses a **watch cache**. The implementation can be found in\\n201: `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/storage/cacher/`.\\n202: \\n203: *   **Initialization:** The cacher first performs a `LIST` to get the current state of all\\n204:     objects and a `ResourceVersion` for that point-in-time. It then starts a `WATCH` from\\n205:     that version to ensure a consistent stream of events.\\n206: *   **Serving from Cache:** Most list and watch requests are served from this in-memory cache, which\\n207:     dramatically reduces the load on etcd. Consistent reads are also served from the\\n208:     cache. This is achieved by first fetching the revision number of the latest write from\\n209:     etcd. The server then ensures the cache is at least that recent\u2014waiting for it to\\n210:     refresh if necessary\u2014before serving the request.\\n211: *   **Fallback to Storage:** If a client request cannot be served from the\\n212:     cache's buffer, the request \\\"falls through\\\" to the underlying etcd storage.\\n213: *   **Bookmarks:** The cacher uses bookmark events to track the latest `ResourceVersion` for\\n214:     unchanged objects. This prevents the cache's `ResourceVersion` from becoming too old,\\n215:     which avoids the need for expensive relist operations from etcd when the objects have\\n216:     not been modified.\\n217: \\n218: ## 5. Conflict Resolution\\n219: \\n220: *   **Optimistic Concurrency via `resourceVersion`:** Clients are expected to perform updates using a\\n221:     read-modify-write workflow. The apiserver uses the `resourceVersion` field of every\\n222:     object to enforce optimistic concurrency. This `resourceVersion` is not an arbitrary number;\\n223:     it maps directly to etcd's globally consistent `mod_revision`. When a client submits an\\n224:     update (`PUT` or `PATCH`), it must provide the `resourceVersion` of the object it based its\\n225:     modifications on. If the `resourceVersion` on the server does not match the current\\n226:     `mod_revision` in etcd, the server rejects the request with a `409 Conflict` error. This\\n227:     forces the client to re-read the object, resolve the conflict, and resubmit with the new\\n228:     `resourceVersion`.\\n229: *   **Server-Side Apply:** A declarative, \\\"intent-based\\\" patch. The server maintains a\\n230:     `managedFields` section in the object's metadata to track which \\\"manager\\\" (e.g., a\\n231:     controller) owns each field. This allows multiple actors to manage different parts of the\\n232:     same object without overwriting each other's changes.\\n233: \\n234: ## 6. Discovery and OpenAPI\\n235: \\n236: Apiservers serve the `/apis` discovery endpoints and the `/openapi/v2` and `/openapi/v3`\\n237: specifications. The generation of the OpenAPI specification is a multi-stage process.\\n238: \\n239: *   **`openapi-gen`**: This tool reflects on Go structs, reads godoc comments, and looks at\\n240:     validation struct tags to generate a map of all API definitions.\\n241: *   **`zz_generated.openapi.go`**: The output is a large Go file containing a\\n242:     `GetOpenAPIDefinitions` function.\\n243: *   **Runtime**: The `GenericAPIServer` calls this generated function to build the final OpenAPI\\n244:     JSON spec that it serves to clients.\\n245: \\n246: ## 7. Security & Observability\\n247: \\n248: *   **Audit (`pkg/audit`):** The apiserver has a policy-driven event logging pipeline. The audit\\n249:     policy controls what is logged and at which stage of a request.\\n250: *   **Security:**\\n251:     *   **mTLS:** The primary authentication mechanism for system components.\\n252:     *   **Service Account Token Issuance:** The `kube-apiserver` acts as an OIDC provider,\\n253:         issuing and validating JWTs for `ServiceAccount`s.\\n254: \\n255: ## 8. Streaming Protocols\\n256: \\n257: *   **Websockets:** The apiserver uses websockets to upgrade HTTP\\n258:     connections for interactive, streaming protocols like `exec`, `attach`, and\\n259:     `port-forward`. The `UpgradeAwareProxyHandler` manages this process.\\n260: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/kubernetes--8c9c67c0@8c9c67c000104450cfc5a5f48053a9a84b73cf93/-/blob/staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/ARCHITECTURE.md\"}",
  "structuredContent": {
    "content": "1: # apiserver Architecture\n2: \n3: ## 1. Server Composition\n4: \n5: The `kube-apiserver` binary is not one server, but a **server chain** of three distinct\n6: `GenericAPIServer` instances: the core Kubernetes API, API extensions (CRDs), and the aggregation\n7: layer.\n8: \n9: This composition is managed by a layered configuration system, starting with command-line\n10: flags parsed by `options` structs (e.g., `RecommendedOptions` in `pkg/server/options`), which\n11: populate a `Config` object that is then used to instantiate the `GenericAPIServer`\n12: instances. The construction of this delegation chain can be found in the `CreateServerChain`\n13: function in `cmd/kube-apiserver/app/server.go`.\n14: \n15: ```mermaid\n16: graph TD\n17:     subgraph Incoming Request\n18:         direction LR\n19:         A[User/Client] --> B{/apis/apps/v1/deployments};\n20:     end\n21: \n22:     subgraph kube-apiserver process\n23:         direction LR\n24:         B --> C[Aggregator Server];\n25:         C -- Not an APIService --> D[Kube API Server];\n26:         D -- Handles Request --> E[REST Storage];\n27:         C -- Is an APIService --> F[Proxy to Extension API Server];\n28:         D -- Not a Core API --> G[API Extensions Server];\n29:         G -- Handles CRD --> E;\n30:     end\n31: ```\n32: \n33: 1.  **Aggregator Server (`kube-aggregator`):**\n34:     *   **Purpose:** Handles the `apiregistration.k8s.io` API and acts as a reverse proxy for\n35:         extension API servers. This functionality was designed to allow third-party APIs to be\n36:         \"aggregated\" into the main Kubernetes API server seamlessly.\n37:     *   **Mechanism:** It watches `APIService` objects. When a request arrives (e.g., for\n38:         `/apis/mycompany.com/v1/myresources`), it checks if an `APIService` has \"claimed\"\n39:         that path. If so, it uses a `ServiceResolver` to find the IP of the backing\n40:         `Service` and proxies the request.\n41:     *   **Use Case:** This pattern is for programmatic, high-control extensions that require\n42:         custom business logic (e.g., non-CRUD subresources like `/logs`) or alternative\n43:         storage backends.\n44:     *   **Delegation:** If no `APIService` matches, it delegates the request to the next\n45:         server in the chain.\n46: \n47: 2.  **Kube API Server (Core):**\n48:     *   **Purpose:** Serves all the built-in Kubernetes APIs (`core/v1`, `apps/v1`, etc.).\n49:     *   **Mechanism:** This is the main server, configured with all the core REST storage\n50:         strategies.\n51:     *   **Delegation:** If a request is for a path that is not a core API (e.g., for a CRD),\n52:         it delegates the request to the next server in the chain.\n53: \n54: 3.  **API Extensions Server (`apiextensions-apiserver`):**\n55:     *   **Purpose:** Handles the `apiextensions.k8s.io` API, which manages\n56:         `CustomResourceDefinition` objects (CRDs). The evolution of CRDs from a simple extension\n57:         mechanism to a feature-rich system with validation, versioning, and defaulting is\n58:         documented in a series of KEPs, starting with the graduation to GA in Kubernetes v1.16.\n59:     *   **Mechanism:** When a CRD is created, this server dynamically creates and installs a\n60:         new REST storage handler for the new resource, making it immediately available.\n61:     *   **Use Case:** CRDs are the most common extension pattern, offering a declarative,\n62:         schema-based way to define new resource types that are stored in etcd and require no\n63:         custom API server code.\n64:     *   **Delegation:** It is the end of the chain. If it cannot handle a request, a `404 Not\n65:         Found` is returned.\n66: \n67: ## 2. Handler Chain\n68: \n69: Every request flows through a standard chain of HTTP handlers (filters). The request body\n70: is not deserialized until it has passed authentication and authorization. The default handler\n71: chain is constructed by the `DefaultBuildHandlerChain` function in\n72: `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/server/config.go`.\n73: \n74: ```mermaid\n75: sequenceDiagram\n76:     participant Client\n77:     participant Handler Chain\n78:     participant Authentication\n79:     participant Authorization\n80:     participant Priority and Fairness\n81:     participant Admission Control\n82:     participant REST Endpoint\n83: \n84:     Client->>Handler Chain: Request\n85:     Handler Chain->>Authentication: Authenticate\n86:     Authentication-->>Handler Chain: User Info\n87:     Handler Chain->>Authorization: Authorize\n88:     Authorization-->>Handler Chain: Allowed/Denied\n89:     Handler Chain->>Priority and Fairness: Classify & Queue\n90:     Priority and Fairness-->>Handler Chain: Proceed\n91:     Handler Chain->>Admission Control: Mutate & Validate\n92:     Admission Control-->>Handler Chain: Object OK\n93:     Handler Chain->>REST Endpoint: Handle\n94:     REST Endpoint-->>Handler Chain: Response\n95:     Handler Chain-->>Client: Response\n96: ```\n97: \n98: The handler chain consists of the following stages:\n99: \n100: 1.  **Authentication (`pkg/authentication`):** This filter identifies the user. The system is\n101:     pluggable and composed of multiple authenticators (e.g., client certs, bearer tokens, OIDC).\n102:     The identity of the user is determined by the first authenticator in the chain that successfully\n103:     identifies the user.\n104: 2.  **Authorization (`pkg/authorization`):** This filter checks if the user is permitted to\n105:     perform the action. This system is also pluggable and composed of multiple authorizers\n106:     (e.g., RBAC, Node, Webhook). Each authorizer may respond with either allow, deny, or no opinion.\n107:     If the response is no opinion, the request is passed to the next authorizer in the chain.\n108: 3.  **Priority and Fairness (`pkg/util/flowcontrol`):** This subsystem manages request\n109:     concurrency, classifying requests into `FlowSchema`s and `PriorityLevel`s to prevent\n110:     overload. This feature was introduced to prevent high traffic from overwhelming the API\n111:     server and to ensure that critical cluster operations are not starved.\n112: 4.  **Admission Control (`pkg/admission`):** This is the primary mechanism for policy\n113:     enforcement. It is only at this stage that the request body is deserialized into an\n114:     object. It is a chain of plugins that can mutate or validate an object. The built-in Pod\n115:     Security admission controller is a key example of this, enforcing Pod Security Standards\n116:     at the namespace level.\n117: 5.  **REST Endpoint Handling (`pkg/endpoints`):** The request is finally dispatched to the\n118:     appropriate REST handler, which is installed by the `APIInstaller`.\n119: \n120: ## 3. API Group Registration\n121: \n122: The high-level steps for introducing an API are:\n123: \n124: 1.  **Define Types:** Create or modify the Go structs in the `types.go` file for the API group.\n125: 2.  **Generate Code:** Use the code generators provided by the Kubernetes project to create the required\n126:     boilerplate methods for deep-copy, conversion, and defaulting.\n127: 3.  **Implement the `Strategy`:** Write the custom business logic and validation for the\n128:     resource in its `Strategy` object.\n129: 4.  **Register and Install:** Create the `APIGroupInfo` struct, bundling the `Scheme` and the\n130:     `Strategy`-configured storage, and pass it to the `GenericAPIServer`'s `InstallAPIGroup`\n131:     method.\n132: \n133: ### The API Group Registry\n134: \n135: The `runtime.Scheme` acts as a central registry for an API group's type information. A single\n136: `Scheme` object is created for each API group and is responsible for the following key\n137: capabilities:\n138: \n139: *   **Type Registration and Mapping:** The `Scheme`'s primary role is to map a GroupVersionKind\n140:     (GVK) to its corresponding Go type and back. This process also relies on the `deepcopy-gen`\n141:     tool to create `DeepCopy()` methods for each type, which is critical for ensuring that\n142:     objects returned from caches are never modified directly.\n143: \n144: *   **API Conversion:** The `Scheme` stores the conversion functions that translate objects\n145:     between different API versions. These functions are typically generated by the\n146:     `conversion-gen` tool and enable the **hub-and-spoke** model.\n147: \n148: *   **Defaulting:** The `Scheme` registers defaulting functions that populate optional fields in\n149:     an object. These are usually generated by the `defaulter-gen` tool.\n150: \n151: *   **Declarative Validation:** The `Scheme` can store and execute code-generated validation\n152:     functions, providing a baseline level of validation. This is distinct from the primary,\n153:     handwritten business logic validation, which is handled by the `Strategy` object.\n154: \n155: ### The `APIGroupInfo` Struct and `Strategy` Object\n156: \n157: With a populated `Scheme`, the API group is registered with the `GenericAPIServer` by bundling\n158: the `Scheme` with the storage backend and versioning information into an `APIGroupInfo` struct.\n159: \n160: ```mermaid\n161: graph TD\n162:     subgraph Server Configuration\n163:         A[APIGroupInfo for apps v1];\n164:         A --> B{Scheme: Knows Deployment v1};\n165:         A --> C{Storage: deployments RESTStorage};\n166:         A --> D{Version Priority: v1, v1beta1};\n167:     end\n168: \n169:     subgraph RESTStorage Implementation\n170:         C --> E[genericregistry.Store];\n171:         E --> F[etcd client];\n172:         E --> G[Deployment Strategy];\n173:     end\n174: \n175:     subgraph Server Runtime\n176:         H[GenericAPIServer] -- InstallAPIGroup --> I[APIInstaller];\n177:         I -- Uses --> A;\n178:         I --> J{Register /apis/apps/v1/deployments};\n179:         J --> K[HTTP Handler];\n180:         K -- On Request --> C;\n181:     end\n182: ```\n183: \n184: The registration process follows these steps:\n185: \n186: 1.  **`APIGroupInfo` Construction:** For each API group, an `APIGroupInfo` struct is created,\n187:     which contains the populated `Scheme`, a map of resources to their storage\n188:     implementations, and an ordered list of **Version Priority**.\n189: \n190: 2.  **REST Storage Instantiation:** For each resource, a `genericregistry.Store` is created. It\n191:     is configured with a resource-specific `Strategy` object that contains the core business\n192:     logic (e.g., handwritten validation).\n193: \n194: 3.  **API Group Installation:** The `GenericAPIServer`'s `InstallAPIGroup` method takes the\n195:     `APIGroupInfo` and uses an `APIInstaller` to expose the resources as HTTP endpoints.\n196: \n197: ## 4. Watch Cache\n198: \n199: To handle the high volume of watch requests from controllers without overwhelming etcd, the\n200: apiserver uses a **watch cache**. The implementation can be found in\n201: `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/storage/cacher/`.\n202: \n203: *   **Initialization:** The cacher first performs a `LIST` to get the current state of all\n204:     objects and a `ResourceVersion` for that point-in-time. It then starts a `WATCH` from\n205:     that version to ensure a consistent stream of events.\n206: *   **Serving from Cache:** Most list and watch requests are served from this in-memory cache, which\n207:     dramatically reduces the load on etcd. Consistent reads are also served from the\n208:     cache. This is achieved by first fetching the revision number of the latest write from\n209:     etcd. The server then ensures the cache is at least that recent\u2014waiting for it to\n210:     refresh if necessary\u2014before serving the request.\n211: *   **Fallback to Storage:** If a client request cannot be served from the\n212:     cache's buffer, the request \"falls through\" to the underlying etcd storage.\n213: *   **Bookmarks:** The cacher uses bookmark events to track the latest `ResourceVersion` for\n214:     unchanged objects. This prevents the cache's `ResourceVersion` from becoming too old,\n215:     which avoids the need for expensive relist operations from etcd when the objects have\n216:     not been modified.\n217: \n218: ## 5. Conflict Resolution\n219: \n220: *   **Optimistic Concurrency via `resourceVersion`:** Clients are expected to perform updates using a\n221:     read-modify-write workflow. The apiserver uses the `resourceVersion` field of every\n222:     object to enforce optimistic concurrency. This `resourceVersion` is not an arbitrary number;\n223:     it maps directly to etcd's globally consistent `mod_revision`. When a client submits an\n224:     update (`PUT` or `PATCH`), it must provide the `resourceVersion` of the object it based its\n225:     modifications on. If the `resourceVersion` on the server does not match the current\n226:     `mod_revision` in etcd, the server rejects the request with a `409 Conflict` error. This\n227:     forces the client to re-read the object, resolve the conflict, and resubmit with the new\n228:     `resourceVersion`.\n229: *   **Server-Side Apply:** A declarative, \"intent-based\" patch. The server maintains a\n230:     `managedFields` section in the object's metadata to track which \"manager\" (e.g., a\n231:     controller) owns each field. This allows multiple actors to manage different parts of the\n232:     same object without overwriting each other's changes.\n233: \n234: ## 6. Discovery and OpenAPI\n235: \n236: Apiservers serve the `/apis` discovery endpoints and the `/openapi/v2` and `/openapi/v3`\n237: specifications. The generation of the OpenAPI specification is a multi-stage process.\n238: \n239: *   **`openapi-gen`**: This tool reflects on Go structs, reads godoc comments, and looks at\n240:     validation struct tags to generate a map of all API definitions.\n241: *   **`zz_generated.openapi.go`**: The output is a large Go file containing a\n242:     `GetOpenAPIDefinitions` function.\n243: *   **Runtime**: The `GenericAPIServer` calls this generated function to build the final OpenAPI\n244:     JSON spec that it serves to clients.\n245: \n246: ## 7. Security & Observability\n247: \n248: *   **Audit (`pkg/audit`):** The apiserver has a policy-driven event logging pipeline. The audit\n249:     policy controls what is logged and at which stage of a request.\n250: *   **Security:**\n251:     *   **mTLS:** The primary authentication mechanism for system components.\n252:     *   **Service Account Token Issuance:** The `kube-apiserver` acts as an OIDC provider,\n253:         issuing and validating JWTs for `ServiceAccount`s.\n254: \n255: ## 8. Streaming Protocols\n256: \n257: *   **Websockets:** The apiserver uses websockets to upgrade HTTP\n258:     connections for interactive, streaming protocols like `exec`, `attach`, and\n259:     `port-forward`. The `UpgradeAwareProxyHandler` manages this process.\n260: ",

{
  "content": "# Kubernetes API Server Library: Architecture and Extension Guide\n\n## Executive Summary\n\nThe `k8s.io/apiserver` library provides the foundational framework for building RESTful API servers that serve Kubernetes resource definitions. It abstracts away the complexity of HTTP request handling, authentication, authorization, admission control, storage management, and watch caching\u2014enabling both the core Kubernetes API server and custom API servers to focus on business logic rather than infrastructure.\n\nThis library is the backbone of Kubernetes' pluggable architecture, supporting three distinct server compositions: the core Kubernetes API server, custom resource definitions (CRDs) through the API extensions server, and aggregated third-party APIs through the aggregation layer.\n\n---\n\n## 1. Subsystem Purpose, Boundaries, and Dependencies\n\n### Purpose\n\nThe apiserver library provides a **request processing pipeline** that transforms raw HTTP requests into Kubernetes object mutations, while enforcing security policies, admission rules, and consistency guarantees. It bridges the gap between RESTful HTTP semantics and Kubernetes' declarative state management model.\n\n### Core Responsibilities\n\n1. **HTTP request routing and dispatch** to appropriate storage backends\n2. **Security enforcement** (authentication, authorization, impersonation)\n3. **Policy enforcement** via admission control plugins\n4. **Resource-agnostic storage abstraction** with pluggable backends (primarily etcd)\n5. **Watch cache management** to reduce load on storage\n6. **Conflict resolution** using optimistic concurrency control\n7. **API versioning and discovery** including OpenAPI schema generation\n\n### Boundaries\n\n**Internal (owned by apiserver):**\n- HTTP filter chain and middleware\n- REST storage interface definitions\n- Watch caching layer\n- Admission control framework\n- Storage encryption and transformation\n- Flow control and request prioritization\n- Audit logging infrastructure\n- OpenAPI discovery endpoints\n\n**External (imported dependencies):**\n- **apimachinery** (`k8s.io/apimachinery`): Type definitions, runtime/scheme management, serialization codecs\n- **client-go** (`k8s.io/client-go`): Client configuration and connectivity\n- **component-base** (`k8s.io/component-base`): Common utilities (logging, feature gates, metrics)\n- **kube-openapi** (`k8s.io/kube-openapi`): OpenAPI v2/v3 spec generation\n- **etcd3 client**: Underlying storage backend communication\n\n**Consumers (direct dependents):**\n- `kube-apiserver` (main Kubernetes API server)\n- `apiextensions-apiserver` (CRD management)\n- `kube-aggregator` (API aggregation/proxying)\n- Any custom API server built from this library\n\n---\n\n## 2. Key Components and Responsibility Split\n\n### 2.1 GenericAPIServer: The Core Orchestrator\n\n**File:** `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/server/genericapiserver.go`\n\nThe `GenericAPIServer` is the central component that orchestrates the entire request handling pipeline. It is responsible for:\n\n- **Holding mutable state** about the server (serving info, routes, health checks)\n- **Installing API groups** via `InstallAPIGroup()` method\n- **Managing the handler chain** through the `APIServerHandler`\n- **Providing loopback authentication** for internal components\n- **Registering hooks** for startup and shutdown lifecycle\n\n```go\ntype GenericAPIServer struct {\n    Handler              *APIServerHandler\n    LoopbackClientConfig *restclient.Config\n    SecureServingInfo    *SecureServingInfo\n    admissionControl     admission.Interface\n    // ... other fields\n}\n```\n\nThe server maintains **pluggable filter chains** but in practice uses the canonical `DefaultBuildHandlerChain` function in most cases.\n\n### 2.2 Config and RecommendedOptions: Configuration Pipeline\n\n**File:** `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/server/config.go`\n\nThe `Config` struct aggregates all initialization parameters for a `GenericAPIServer`. Key fields include:\n\n- **Security:** `SecureServing`, `Authentication`, `Authorization`\n- **Admission:** `AdmissionControl`\n- **Storage:** `RESTOptionsGetter`, `StorageVersionManager`\n- **Flow Control:** `FlowControl` (for request prioritization)\n- **Resource Config:** `MergedResourceConfig` (which APIs/versions are enabled)\n- **Hooks:** `BuildHandlerChainFunc`, `PostStartHooks`\n\nThe library also provides `RecommendedOptions` as a convenience structure that bundles common configurations with their command-line flag parsing. This is the primary entrypoint for server builders:\n\n```go\ntype RecommendedConfig struct {\n    Config\n    SharedInformerFactory informers.SharedInformerFactory\n    ClientConfig          *restclient.Config\n}\n```\n\n### 2.3 APIGroupInfo: API Group Registration Bundle\n\n**File:** `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/server/config.go` (lines 71-98)\n\nEach API group is registered with a `GenericAPIServer` through an `APIGroupInfo` struct that packages together:\n\n- **`Scheme`:** Runtime type registry (GVK \u2194 Go type mapping)\n- **`VersionedResourcesStorageMap`:** Maps version \u2192 resource name \u2192 storage backend\n- **`NegotiatedSerializer`:** Encodes/decodes objects to wire formats\n- **`ParameterCodec`:** Converts URL query parameters to Go types\n- **`PrioritizedVersions`:** Ordered list of API versions for preference\n\n```go\ntype APIGroupInfo struct {\n    PrioritizedVersions           []schema.GroupVersion\n    VersionedResourcesStorageMap  map[string]map[string]rest.Storage\n    Scheme                        *runtime.Scheme\n    NegotiatedSerializer          runtime.NegotiatedSerializer\n    ParameterCodec                runtime.ParameterCodec\n}\n```\n\nThe `InstallAPIGroup()` method (in `genericapiserver.go`) uses this to register HTTP routes via the `APIInstaller`.\n\n### 2.4 Handler Chain Architecture: Request Flow Through Filters\n\n**File:** `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/server/config.go` (lines 1015-1097)\n\nThe **request filter chain** is the heart of the apiserver's request processing. The canonical chain (from `DefaultBuildHandlerChain`) applies filters in this order:\n\n1. **AuditInit** \u2013 Initialize audit context\n2. **PanicRecovery** \u2013 Catch panics before they crash the server\n3. **MuxAndDiscoveryComplete** \u2013 Wait for mux initialization\n4. **RequestReceivedTimestamp** \u2013 Record when request arrived\n5. **RequestInfo** \u2013 Parse request path/method into structured info\n6. **WithRoutine** (optional) \u2013 Execute remaining chain in separate goroutine\n7. **HTTPLogging** \u2013 Log requests\n8. **LatencyTrackers** \u2013 Track per-filter latency\n9. **RetryAfter** (optional) \u2013 Return 429 during graceful shutdown\n10. **HSTS** \u2013 Add HTTP Strict-Transport-Security header\n11. **ProbabilisticGoaway** (optional) \u2013 Send HTTP/2 GOAWAY for connection rebalancing\n12. **CacheControl** \u2013 Add cache headers\n13. **WaitGroup** \u2013 Track completion for graceful shutdown\n14. **WatchTerminationDuringShutdown** (optional) \u2013 Wait for watch requests during shutdown\n15. **RequestDeadline** \u2013 Enforce timeout and audit deadline\n16. **TimeoutForNonLongRunning** \u2013 Return timeout error if request exceeds limit\n17. **WarningRecorder** \u2013 Add deprecation warnings\n18. **CORS** \u2013 Add Cross-Origin headers\n19. **Authentication** \u2013 Identify user (client cert, bearer token, etc.)\n20. **Tracing** (optional) \u2013 Record spans for distributed tracing\n21. **FailedAuthenticationAudit** \u2013 Audit failed auth attempts\n22. **Audit** \u2013 Log request before processing\n23. **Impersonation** \u2013 Handle user/group/UID impersonation\n24. **ConstrainedImpersonation** (optional) \u2013 Limit impersonation scope\n25. **MaxInFlightLimit** or **PriorityAndFairness** \u2013 Admission queue\n26. **Authorization** \u2013 Check if user can perform action\n27. **StorageVersionPrecondition** (optional) \u2013 Enforce storage API version\n\nEach filter is composed with the next using standard Go HTTP middleware pattern.\n\n### 2.5 REST Storage Layer: Pluggable Backends\n\n**File:** `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/registry/rest/rest.go`\n\nThe `rest.Storage` interface defines a minimal contract that any storage backend must implement:\n\n```go\ntype Storage interface {\n    New() runtime.Object      // Create empty object for request\n    Destroy()                 // Cleanup on shutdown\n}\n```\n\nResources implement optional interfaces for CRUD operations:\n\n- **`Lister`** \u2013 List objects matching query (implements `/resources`)\n- **`Getter`** \u2013 Retrieve single object (implements `/resources/{name}`)\n- **`Creater`** \u2013 Create new object (implements POST)\n- **`GracefulDeleter`** \u2013 Delete object (implements DELETE)\n- **`Updater` / `Patcher`** \u2013 Modify object (implements PUT/PATCH)\n- **`Watcher`** \u2013 Stream changes (implements WATCH)\n\nMost Kubernetes resources use `genericregistry.Store` which wraps an etcd backend, but custom resources can implement alternative storage.\n\n### 2.6 Strategy Objects: Business Logic Container\n\n**File:** Referenced in `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/registry/generic/registry/store.go` (lines 501, 753)\n\nFor each resource type, a `Strategy` object encapsulates resource-specific business logic:\n\n- **`BeforeCreate(ctx, obj)`** \u2013 Mutate/validate object before storage\n- **`ValidateCreate(ctx, obj)`** \u2013 Validate object on CREATE\n- **`BeforeUpdate(ctx, obj, oldObj)`** \u2013 Mutate/validate on UPDATE\n- **`ValidateUpdate(ctx, obj, oldObj)`** \u2013 Validate on UPDATE\n- **`Canonicalize(obj)`** \u2013 Normalize object representation\n\nStrategies run **before admission control** deserialization, providing a low-level hook for enforcement.\n\n### 2.7 Admission Control Framework: Policy Layer\n\n**File:** `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/admission/interfaces.go`\n\nThe admission system is a chain of pluggable controllers. Each implements one or both interfaces:\n\n```go\ntype MutationInterface interface {\n    Interface\n    Admit(ctx context.Context, a Attributes, o ObjectInterfaces) error\n}\n\ntype ValidationInterface interface {\n    Interface\n    Validate(ctx context.Context, a Attributes, o ObjectInterfaces) error\n}\n```\n\nAdmission plugins receive the deserialized object and can:\n- **Mutate** the object (with `MutationInterface`)\n- **Validate** the object (with `ValidationInterface`)\n- **Prevent** the operation entirely by returning an error\n- **Audit** the decision via annotations\n\nKey built-in plugins include:\n- **PodSecurityPolicy** \u2013 Enforce pod security constraints\n- **ResourceQuota** \u2013 Enforce namespace quotas\n- **LimitRanger** \u2013 Enforce resource limits\n- **ValidatingWebhook** \u2013 Call external validation services\n- **MutatingWebhook** \u2013 Call external mutation services\n\nAdmission runs **after deserialization** but **before storage**, making it the primary point for policy enforcement.\n\n### 2.8 Watch Cache Layer: Performance Optimization\n\n**File:** `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/storage/cacher/cacher.go`\n\nThe watch cache (`Cacher` type) intercepts storage requests to serve reads from an in-memory buffer instead of etcd when possible.\n\n**Initialization:**\n1. Performs initial `LIST` operation to get current state and `ResourceVersion`\n2. Starts a `WATCH` from that version to maintain consistent event stream\n3. Buffers watched events in a circular buffer (~1000 events by default)\n\n**Serving requests:**\n- Most `LIST` and `WATCH` requests are served from cache\n- Consistent reads first fetch latest etcd revision, then wait for cache to catch up\n- If cache buffer is full, requests \"fall through\" to underlying storage\n\n**Key optimization: Bookmarks**\n- Cacher sends periodic \"bookmark\" events tracking the latest `ResourceVersion`\n- This prevents clients' watch streams from becoming stale\n- Prevents expensive re-LIST operations\n\nThe cacher is **transparent** to storage consumers but dramatically reduces etcd load in typical clusters (90%+ of requests can be served from cache).\n\n### 2.9 APIInstaller: Endpoint Registration\n\n**File:** `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/endpoints/installer.go`\n\nThe `APIInstaller` takes an `APIGroupInfo` and registers HTTP endpoints. For each resource, it creates routes for:\n\n- `GET /apis/group/v1/resources` (List)\n- `POST /apis/group/v1/resources` (Create)\n- `GET /apis/group/v1/resources/{name}` (Get)\n- `PUT /apis/group/v1/resources/{name}` (Update)\n- `PATCH /apis/group/v1/resources/{name}` (Patch)\n- `DELETE /apis/group/v1/resources/{name}` (Delete)\n- `WATCH /apis/group/v1/resources` (Watch)\n\nFor namespaced resources, it also registers namespace-scoped variants.\n\nSubresources (e.g., `/status`, `/logs`) are registered separately if the resource's storage backend implements the `SubResourceStorage` interface.\n\n---\n\n## 3. End-to-End Request Flow with Concrete Evidence\n\n### 3.1 Anatomy of a PUT Request to `/apis/apps/v1/namespaces/default/deployments/my-app`\n\n#### Phase 1: Filter Chain (Middleware)\n\n```\nHTTP Request \u2192 PanicRecovery\n             \u2192 RequestInfo (extracts: group=apps, version=v1, resource=deployments, name=my-app, namespace=default)\n             \u2192 Authentication (identifies user via cert/token)\n             \u2192 Audit (logs intention to modify)\n             \u2192 Impersonation (applies user override if specified)\n             \u2192 PriorityAndFairness (queues if overloaded)\n             \u2192 Authorization (RBAC check: can user modify deployments/my-app?)\n             \u2192 ValidatingWebhook (call external validation)\n             \u2192 REST Handler\n```\n\n**Source:** `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/server/config.go` lines 1015-1097 show this chain.\n\n#### Phase 2: Object Deserialization and Admission\n\nThe REST handler in `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/endpoints/handlers/` receives the request:\n\n1. **Read and deserialize request body** using `NegotiatedSerializer.Decoder`\n2. **Apply defaults** via `Scheme.DefaulterFuncs`\n3. **Run pre-admission strategy validation** via `Strategy.BeforeUpdate()`\n4. **Invoke admission plugins:**\n   - Mutating plugins can modify the object\n   - Re-invoke mutating plugins if any modified the object (reinvocation)\n   - Validating plugins can reject the object\n\n#### Phase 3: Storage and Conflict Resolution\n\nOnce admitted, the REST handler calls the storage backend's `Update()` method:\n\n```go\n// From staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/registry/generic/registry/store.go\nupdated, _, err := e.Storage.Update(...)\n```\n\nFor `genericregistry.Store` with etcd:\n\n1. **Fetch current object** from storage (checks `resourceVersion` in request matches etcd `mod_revision`)\n2. **If mismatch:** Return 409 Conflict (optimistic concurrency control)\n3. **If match:** Apply transformation pipeline\n   - Run `Strategy.ValidateUpdate()`\n   - Apply resource transformers (encryption, serialization)\n4. **Write to etcd** with new `mod_revision`\n5. **Return updated object** with new `resourceVersion`\n\n**Conflict resolution source:** The `Update` method in `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/registry/generic/registry/store.go` validates `resourceVersion` before applying changes.\n\n#### Phase 4: Watch Notification\n\nOnce storage completes, the write propagates to the watch cache (`Cacher`):\n\n1. **Watch handler in etcd client** receives the change event\n2. **Cacher appends event** to circular buffer\n3. **Active watch requests** receive the event via WebSocket/chunked HTTP\n\n**Source:** `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/storage/cacher/watch_cache.go` maintains the event buffer.\n\n#### Phase 5: Response and Serialization\n\nThe REST handler serializes the updated object:\n\n```go\n// Encode response\nencoder := codecs.Encoder(responseContentType)\nencoder.Encode(updated, w)\n```\n\nThe `NegotiatedSerializer` writes JSON/Protobuf/CBOR based on client's `Accept` header.\n\n---\n\n## 4. Failure Modes, Limits, and Design Tradeoffs\n\n### 4.1 Failure Modes\n\n#### Storage Unavailability\n**Symptom:** Requests hang or return 500 errors\n**Cause:** etcd is down or unreachable\n**Impact:** API server cannot read or write objects\n**Mitigation:** Health checks at `/livez` and `/readyz` detect this; Kubernetes replaces the pod\n\n#### Admission Control Webhook Timeout\n**Symptom:** Create/update requests timeout\n**Cause:** ValidatingWebhook or MutatingWebhook is slow or hanging\n**Behavior:** Request waits up to 30 seconds (default) for webhook response\n**Mitigation:** Webhooks must implement proper timeout handling; admission policies control timeout values\n\n**Source:** Webhook timeout enforcement is in `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/admission/plugin/webhook/` (not shown in exploration but referenced in admission framework)\n\n#### Watch Cache Corruption\n**Symptom:** Watch events are missing or out-of-order\n**Cause:** Cacher's event buffer is corrupted or falls too far behind\n**Impact:** Watch clients miss important events\n**Mitigation:** Cacher implements compaction (`staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/storage/cacher/compactor.go`) and fallback to LIST for recovery\n\n#### Optimistic Concurrency Conflicts\n**Symptom:** Clients receive `409 Conflict` on updates\n**Cause:** Another client modified the object between read and write\n**Behavior:** Expected and normal; client must retry with new `resourceVersion`\n**Impact:** Clients must implement retry logic with backoff\n\n### 4.2 Resource Limits\n\n#### MaxRequestsInFlight / MaxMutatingRequestsInFlight\n- **Default:** 128 read, 64 write (tunable via flags)\n- **Behavior:** Additional requests are queued or rejected if limits exceeded\n- **Source:** `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/server/config.go` lines 254-259\n\n#### MaxRequestBodyBytes\n- **Default:** 0 (unlimited)\n- **Behavior:** Requests larger than this are rejected during deserialization\n- **Use case:** Prevents huge uploads from consuming memory\n\n#### FlowControl (Priority and Fairness)\n- **Concept:** Classifies requests into `FlowSchema` + `PriorityLevel`\n- **Impact:** Prevents high-traffic workloads from starving critical operations\n- **Tuning:** Via `FlowControl` config in `Config` struct\n\n**Source:** `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/util/flowcontrol/` implements flow control\n\n#### Watch Cache Buffer Size\n- **Default:** ~1000 events per resource\n- **Behavior:** Once full, new events evict oldest; clients must fall back to LIST\n- **Tuning:** Not directly exposed in public API; hardcoded in cacher implementation\n\n### 4.3 Design Tradeoffs\n\n#### Tradeoff 1: Optimistic Concurrency vs. Pessimistic Locking\n- **Chosen:** Optimistic (resourceVersion-based)\n- **Advantage:** Allows concurrent reads without locks\n- **Disadvantage:** Clients must handle and retry conflicts\n- **Evidence:** ARCHITECTURE.md section 5 explains conflict resolution\n\n#### Tradeoff 2: Admission Before vs. After Deserialization\n- **Chosen:** Admission runs **after** deserialization\n- **Advantage:** Plugins can inspect full object structure\n- **Disadvantage:** Malformed payloads cause errors before admission\n- **Evidence:** Admission runs in REST handler chain (after `RequestInfo`), but `deserializeRequest` happens inside handler\n\n#### Tradeoff 3: Watch Cache Eventual Consistency\n- **Chosen:** Cache is eventually consistent (not strongly consistent)\n- **Advantage:** Reduces etcd load dramatically (90%+ requests served from cache)\n- **Disadvantage:** Clients may see stale data if they immediately read after write\n- **Mitigation:** Clients can use `resourceVersion` consistency hint\n- **Evidence:** `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/storage/cacher/cacher.go` maintains eventual consistency with bookmarks\n\n#### Tradeoff 4: REST Serialization Overhead\n- **Chosen:** Support multiple codecs (JSON, Protobuf, CBOR)\n- **Advantage:** Wire efficiency; clients choose format\n- **Disadvantage:** Serialization/deserialization CPU overhead\n- **Tuning:** Can be optimized with specialized codecs per resource\n\n---\n\n## 5. Extension Points, Customization Hooks, and Associated Risks\n\n### 5.1 Authentication Extension Points\n\n**Location:** `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/authentication/`\n\n**Hook:** `Config.Authentication.Authenticator`\n\n**How it works:**\n- Implement `authenticator.Request` interface\n- Register via authenticator factory\n- Chain multiple authenticators (first one to succeed wins)\n\n**Built-in Options:**\n- Client certificate\n- Bearer token (static, service account, OIDC)\n- Basic auth (deprecated)\n- Custom webhook\n\n**Risk:** Authenticators that fail open (always authenticate) bypass all security\n\n---\n\n### 5.2 Authorization Extension Points\n\n**Location:** `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/authorization/`\n\n**Hook:** `Config.Authorization.Authorizer`\n\n**How it works:**\n- Implement `authorizer.Authorizer` interface\n- Chain multiple authorizers (all must allow or first to deny wins)\n- Can return \"no opinion\" to pass to next authorizer\n\n**Built-in Options:**\n- RBAC (role-based access control)\n- ABAC (attribute-based, deprecated)\n- Node (kubelet-specific)\n- Webhook (external authorizer)\n- AlwaysAllow (for testing only)\n\n**Risk:** Authorizers with permissive defaults can grant unintended access\n\n---\n\n### 5.3 Admission Control Extension Points\n\n**Location:** `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/admission/`\n\n**Hook:** `Config.AdmissionControl` (chain of `Interface` implementations)\n\n**Plugin architecture:**\n\n```go\n// Implement both interfaces or just one:\ntype MyPlugin struct { }\n\nfunc (p *MyPlugin) Admit(ctx context.Context, a Attributes, o ObjectInterfaces) error {\n    // Mutate object here\n    return nil\n}\n\nfunc (p *MyPlugin) Validate(ctx context.Context, a Attributes, o ObjectInterfaces) error {\n    // Validate object here\n    return nil\n}\n```\n\n**Built-in plugins:**\n- ValidatingWebhook / MutatingWebhook\n- PodSecurityPolicy\n- ResourceQuota\n- LimitRanger\n- ServiceAccount\n- DefaultStorageClass\n- And many more\n\n**Extension mechanism:** ValidatingWebhook and MutatingWebhook allow external services to implement admission logic\n\n**Risk:** Admission plugins have full access to object payload and user info; poorly written plugins can leak secrets or cause DOS\n\n**Risk:** Slow or timeout-prone plugins block all requests\n\n---\n\n### 5.4 Storage Backend Extension Points\n\n**Location:** `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/registry/rest/`\n\n**Hook:** `Config.RESTOptionsGetter`\n\n**How it works:**\n- Implement `genericregistry.RESTOptionsGetter` interface\n- Returns `REST storage` for each resource (typically wraps etcd)\n- Can implement custom storage via `rest.Storage` interface\n\n**Built-in:** `etcd3.store` (wrapper around etcd v3 client)\n\n**Example extension:** Custom SQL-backed storage\n```go\ntype MySQLStorage struct { /* fields */ }\n\nfunc (m *MySQLStorage) Create(ctx context.Context, ...) (runtime.Object, error) {\n    // Query MySQL instead of etcd\n}\n\nfunc (m *MySQLStorage) List(ctx context.Context, ...) (runtime.Object, error) {\n    // SELECT from MySQL\n}\n```\n\n**Risk:** Implementing storage incorrectly can lose data or violate consistency guarantees\n\n**Risk:** Alternative storage backends may not support watches, requiring fallback implementations\n\n---\n\n### 5.5 Transformation / Encryption Extension Points\n\n**Location:** `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/storage/value/`\n\n**Hook:** `Config.ResourceTransformers`\n\n**How it works:**\n- Implement `Transformer` interface\n- Applied to every object before storage and after retrieval\n- Can encrypt/compress/hash sensitive fields\n\n**Use case:** Encrypt secrets in etcd\n\n**Risk:** Incorrect transformer can corrupt data or prevent recovery\n\n---\n\n### 5.6 Filter Chain Extension Points\n\n**Location:** `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/server/config.go`\n\n**Hook:** `Config.BuildHandlerChainFunc`\n\n**How it works:**\n- Allows complete override of the filter chain\n- Called during server initialization\n- Can wrap the default chain or replace it entirely\n\n**Pattern:**\n```go\nconfig.BuildHandlerChainFunc = func(apiHandler http.Handler, c *Config) http.Handler {\n    handler := apiHandler\n    handler = myCustomFilter(handler)\n    handler = DefaultBuildHandlerChain(handler, c)\n    return handler\n}\n```\n\n**Risk:** Filters applied before authentication expose user info to untrusted code\n\n---\n\n### 5.7 PostStartHook Extension Points\n\n**Location:** `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/server/genericapiserver.go`\n\n**Hook:** `Config.PostStartHooks`\n\n**How it works:**\n- Callbacks invoked after server starts listening\n- Can initialize controllers, leaders, or other components\n- If a hook fails, the server reports as not ready\n\n**Use case:** Start informers, establish connections to external services\n\n**Risk:** Long-running hooks delay server readiness; timeouts can cause startup failures\n\n---\n\n### 5.8 HealthCheck Extension Points\n\n**Location:** `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/server/healthz/`\n\n**Hook:** `Config.HealthzChecks`, `Config.LivezChecks`, `Config.ReadyzChecks`\n\n**How it works:**\n- Custom `HealthChecker` implementations\n- Called periodically by health check handlers\n- Used by Kubernetes to determine pod readiness\n\n**Example:**\n```go\ntype MyCheck struct { }\nfunc (m *MyCheck) Name() string { return \"mycheck\" }\nfunc (m *MyCheck) Check(req *http.Request) error {\n    // Check custom condition\n    return nil\n}\n\nconfig.AddHealthChecks(mycheck)\n```\n\n**Risk:** Failing health checks can cause unnecessary pod restarts\n\n---\n\n## 6. Operational Implications\n\n### 6.1 Performance and Scalability\n\n#### Request Throughput Bottleneck: Watch Cache Hit Ratio\n- **Fact:** Watch cache serves ~90-95% of list/watch requests in production clusters\n- **Impact:** Cache misses cause direct etcd load; cluster degrades nonlinearly\n- **Monitoring:** Observe etcd operation latencies and request counts\n- **Tuning:** Increase `--cache-size-mb` if etcd is becoming bottleneck (requires restart)\n\n**Location:** Cache size is hardcoded in `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/storage/cacher/watch_cache.go`; heap allocation is tuned per cacher instance created during storage initialization.\n\n#### Admission Control Latency\n- **Fact:** Validating/Mutating webhooks add 10-100ms per request\n- **Impact:** Total request latency = apiserver filters + storage write + webhook delay\n- **Monitoring:** Watch for p99 latencies; webhook call counts\n- **Tuning:** Webhook timeout (30s default) can be lowered; disable non-critical webhooks\n\n#### Memory Footprint\n- **Watch cache buffers:** ~1KB per event \u00d7 number of resources \u00d7 event history\n- **Goroutines:** One per active request + watch stream\n- **Impact:** Large clusters (10k+ pods) can consume 2-4 GB in a single apiserver replica\n\n**Mitigation:**\n- Run multiple apiserver replicas behind load balancer\n- Use `--max-requests-inflight` to limit concurrent requests\n- Monitor goroutine count and heap size via `/metrics`\n\n### 6.2 Reliability and High Availability\n\n#### Graceful Shutdown\n- **Mechanism:** Server waits for in-flight requests to complete before exiting\n- **Timeout:** `Config.ShutdownTimeout` (default 15 seconds)\n- **Behavior:** New requests rejected with 429 Unavailable after `--shutdown-delay-duration`\n- **Operational impact:** Orchestrators should allow at least 30 seconds for graceful termination\n\n**Evidence:** `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/server/genericapiserver.go` manages shutdown signals.\n\n#### Watch Stream Reliability\n- **Issue:** Long-running watch connections consume memory; reconnections cause thundering herd\n- **Mitigation:** Clients should reconnect with exponential backoff; apiserver sends GOAWAY periodically\n- **Configuration:** `--goaway-chance` controls GOAWAY probability (0.01 = 1% chance per connection)\n\n**Source:** Filter at line 1075-1076 in `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/server/config.go` implements GOAWAY.\n\n#### Leader Election for Control Plane Components\n- **Pattern:** Components acquire a `Lease` object via atomic CREATE or UPDATE\n- **Dependency:** Requires working apiserver and etcd; no built-in circuit breaker\n- **Risk:** Cascading failures if apiserver becomes slow\n\n### 6.3 Security and Observability\n\n#### Audit Logging\n- **Mechanism:** Every request flows through audit filter (line 1043 in config.go)\n- **Levels:** None, Metadata, RequestResponse, RequestResponseWithPII\n- **Use case:** Compliance, forensics, security monitoring\n- **Performance:** Audit logging adds latency; async backends (webhook) can cause memory buildup\n\n**Configuration:** `Config.AuditPolicyRuleEvaluator` controls what is logged.\n\n#### Distributed Tracing\n- **Mechanism:** Optional tracing filter (lines 1051-1053) records spans\n- **Benefit:** Understand latency bottlenecks; correlate requests across services\n- **Implementation:** Uses `TracerProvider` (OpenTelemetry-compatible)\n\n#### Metrics\n- **Exported via `/metrics`:** Request latency, count, errors per endpoint\n- **Key metrics:**\n  - `apiserver_request_duration_seconds` (histogram)\n  - `apiserver_request_total` (counter by verb, resource)\n  - `apiserver_currently_inflight_requests`\n  - `etcd_request_duration_seconds` (storage backend latency)\n\n---\n\n## 7. Component Interaction Map\n\n```\nClient HTTP Request\n Summary: Key Takeaways for Extensibility\n\n1. **Plugin via interfaces, not inheritance** \u2013 All extension points use Go interfaces (Authenticator, Authorizer, Storage, etc.)\n\n2. **Request flow is deterministic** \u2013 Filters execute in fixed order; understanding `DefaultBuildHandlerChain` is essential\n\n3. **Admission is the policy layer** \u2013 Use admission plugins (especially webhooks) for business logic; don't modify core storage\n\n4. **Watch cache is non-optional** \u2013 Production deployments depend on 90%+ cache hit rate; storage implementation details matter\n\n5. **Error handling is critical** \u2013 Admission timeouts, storage failures, and admission rejections must be handled gracefully by clients\n\n6. **Operational defaults are production-ready** \u2013 Don't change `--max-requests-inflight`, cache size, or timeout values without load testing\n\n7. **Security is layered** \u2013 Authentication \u2192 Authorization \u2192 Admission; no single point is sufficient; all layers are needed\n\n---\n\n## 10. Architectural Decision Records\n\n### ADR-1: Why Optimistic Concurrency?\n- **Context:** API server must handle concurrent updates to the same object\n- **Options:** Pessimistic locking, optimistic concurrency control, operational transformation\n- **Decision:** Optimistic (via `resourceVersion`)\n- **Rationale:** Allows unbounded concurrent reads; clients are responsible for retry logic\n- **Implication:** Clients must understand and handle conflict errors\n\n### ADR-2: Why Separate Server Chain?\n- **Context:** kube-apiserver must handle core APIs, CRDs, and aggregated APIs\n- **Options:** Single handler, routing-based delegation, separate servers\n- **Decision:** Separate GenericAPIServer instances in delegation chain\n- **Rationale:** Each server can have independent storage, admission rules, and lifecycle\n- **Implication:** More complex initialization; easier to test components independently\n\n### ADR-3: Why Watch Cache?\n- **Context:** Hundreds of controllers watch Pod/Service/Endpoint changes\n- **Options:** Direct etcd access, caching layer, ephemeral caches\n- **Decision:** Central cacher per resource with circular buffer\n- **Rationale:** Reduces etcd load from O(n*m) to O(n) where n=resources, m=watchers\n- **Implication:** Eventual consistency model; clients must handle stale data\n\n",
